<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_083e971a-f8ce-4cd4-a5f0-70f4047061a3'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

</script>

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2272292297535412687">In the dazzling new thriller from the master of dark suspense, the hand of fate reaches out to touch an ordinary man with greatness. So long as he is ready. So long as he is, above all, afraid.<br/><br/>Jimmy Tock comes into the world on the very night his grandfather leaves it. As a violent storm rages outside the hospital, Rudy Tock spends long hours walking the corridors between </span>
  <span id="freeText2272292297535412687" style="display:none">In the dazzling new thriller from the master of dark suspense, the hand of fate reaches out to touch an ordinary man with greatness. So long as he is ready. So long as he is, above all, afraid.<br /><br />Jimmy Tock comes into the world on the very night his grandfather leaves it. As a violent storm rages outside the hospital, Rudy Tock spends long hours walking the corridors between the expectant fathers' waiting room and his dying father's bedside. It's a strange vigil made all the stranger when, at the very height of the storm's fury, Josef Tock suddenly sits up in bed and speaks coherently for the first and last time since his stroke.<br /><br />What he says before he dies is that there will be five dark days in the life of his grandson – five dates whose terrible events Jimmy will have to prepare himself to face. The first is to occur in his 20th year; the second in his 23rd year; the third in his 28th; the fourth in his 29th; the fifth in his 30th.<br /><br />Rudy is all too ready to discount his father's last words as a dying man's delusional rambling. But then he discovers that Josef also predicted the moment of his grandson's birth to the minute, as well as his exact height, weight, and the fact that Jimmy would be born with syndactyly – the unexplained anomaly of fused digits on his left foot. Suddenly, the old man's predictions take on a chilling significance.<br /><br />What terrifying events await Jimmy on these five dark days? What nightmares will he face? What challenges must he survive? As the novel unfolds, picking up Jimmy's story at each of these crisis points, the path he must follow will defy every expectation. And with each crisis he faces, he will move closer to a fate he could never have imagined. For who Jimmy Tock is and what he must accomplish on the five days his world turns is a mystery as dangerous as it is wondrous – a struggle against an evil so dark and pervasive only the most extraordinary of human spirits can shine through.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2272292297535412687" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10064726?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10064726" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6040697801170307074"><b>
  <u>My second favorite Koontz' novel!!!</u>
</b><br/><br/><br/><b>
  <u>A TRUE FAVORITE</u>
</b><br/><br/>I love this book!!!<br/><br/>This novel isn't featured in my virtual shelf of "Favorites", but not because I don't like it, just since I already have <i>Phantoms</i> in that shelf and I want to keep it to maximum 10 books there, it would have to be 2 books from Dean Koontz and if I have to make the hard choice, I'd choose <i>Phantoms</i>.<br/><br/>But, <i>Life Expectancy</i> is a <b>REALLY CLOSE</b> second favorite book by Dean Koontz.<br/><br/>It's one of the best books that I ever read.<br/><br/><br/><b>
  <u>NOT </u></b></span>
  <span id="freeText6040697801170307074" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>My second favorite Koontz' novel!!!</u>
</b><br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>A TRUE FAVORITE</u>
</b><br /><br />I love this book!!!<br /><br />This novel isn't featured in my virtual shelf of "Favorites", but not because I don't like it, just since I already have <i>Phantoms</i> in that shelf and I want to keep it to maximum 10 books there, it would have to be 2 books from Dean Koontz and if I have to make the hard choice, I'd choose <i>Phantoms</i>.<br /><br />But, <i>Life Expectancy</i> is a <b>REALLY CLOSE</b> second favorite book by Dean Koontz.<br /><br />It's one of the best books that I ever read.<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>NOT HORROR PER SE BUT STILL SUPERB</u>
</b><br /><br />It has all, horror, humor, romance, etc... <br /><br />Even it's not your typical horror book, since not matter you have an insane killer here, you don't feel it as in other horror books.<br /><br />If you like the type of books of Stephen King where he develops seudo-paranormal stories with content about life and human relationships, not exactly looking to present a horror story but a complex tale with some horror-like elements on it.<br /><br />I think you can have a pretty good idea of what you will find here.<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>SOOO GOOD!!!</u>
</b><br /><br />Two of the highest points of the books are:<br /><br /><u>First:</u> The humor. You will laugh a lot with this book, because even some situations of life or death are portraited in such surrealistic way that you have to laugh (in a good way) about how the events are ocurring.<br /><br /><u>Second:</u> The female supportive character. She is one that the lead character met. She is fantastic! It's one of the best female characters that I ever read in book. I just love her. I won't elaborate more since I don't want to spoil about her. I just want to say that she is the light who shines plenty, since she becomes part of the story. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6040697801170307074" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_871396978" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/871396978" />
    <a title="Edward Lorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn"><img alt="Edward Lorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492199506p2/8850418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/871396978?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Lorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Lorn" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn">Edward Lorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345533388" href="/book/show/12775707-life-expectancy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer871396978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3715713450389731876">LIFE EXPECTANCY Review I'm about to spoil the hell out of this piece of garbage in the hopes that you run screaming from its pages as if the longevity of your genitals depended on it. <br/> <br/>The Good: This book is readable. Meaning, you can read it. Moving on.<br/> <br/>The Bad: The synopsis is terrific: engaging, intriguing, enticing, everything a book description should be. So why isn't this up there with The Good? Because the synopsis fucking lies like a rug under a steam roller. You see, Jimmy Tock's grandp</span>
  <span id="freeText3715713450389731876" style="display:none">LIFE EXPECTANCY Review I'm about to spoil the hell out of this piece of garbage in the hopes that you run screaming from its pages as if the longevity of your genitals depended on it. <br /> <br />The Good: This book is readable. Meaning, you can read it. Moving on.<br /> <br />The Bad: The synopsis is terrific: engaging, intriguing, enticing, everything a book description should be. So why isn't this up there with The Good? Because the synopsis fucking lies like a rug under a steam roller. You see, Jimmy Tock's grandpappy predicted some shit on his deathbed. Supposedly, his grandson is to have five terribly, horrible, no good, very bad days. The thing is, Koontz spends the entire novel finding ways around these five days, in turn rendering the grandfather's predictions utterly useless. Bad shit happens, mind you, just not on those days. The worst stuff happens on the day before. You might think that would make the book unpredictable, but after the first date, the book is as see through as the glass in a Windex factory. What would have made this book epic is if Koontz had managed to actually achieve what the packaging promises, therein surprising us with unforeseeable twists which occur on the days Deathbed Granddaddy predicted. But no. Not Koontz. He aims for the mundane and nails that motherfucker between the eyes. <br /> <br />The Ugly: LIFE EXPECTANCY is filled with your average meandering Koontz: verbose descriptions of everyday bullshit sprinkled with brief glimpses of interesting detritus buried under an insane knowledge of pastry and off-the-wall diseases he spends nine pages explaining. The writing is so light and dense at the same time it might as well be a Cronut. The words breeze by because Koontz has been doing this shit for nigh on four decades, but no matter how hard he tries, I will never, ever, evereverevereverever, enjoy a detailed examination of goddamn baked goods.<br /> <br />The Unforgivable: Let me preface this bit by saying I've never given a readable book less than two stars. Why? Because the author put enough words together properly so that I might be able to understand what they are saying. I reserve one-star ratings for unedited garbage written by illiterate monkeys with a penchant for banging on typewriters. Well, Dean, you fucking did it, mister. You managed to make me hate a well-written book so much that I cannot in good faith give you anything over a single, solitary, emotionally-crippled star. This book lacks everything even remotely resembling character development. I didn't give a shit about Jimmy, his wife What's-Her-Fuck, the three mostly-forgotten-about children (one of which comes down with cancer in the later part of the book, to which I responded, "Who are you, and who gives a fuck?"), the stupid clown family, the insane aerialists, or even the death of some innocent old lady, because they're all stick figures caricatures of another one of Koontz protagonists from a completely different book. Yes, everyone in this novel is witty and sarcastic. Basically, the cast is overrun with unlikable and forgettable Odd Thomases. <br /> <br />But wait, there's more! The plot, for lack of a better word, becomes so convoluted in the last one hundred pages that I think my brain seeped from my ear to vacation in Aruba when Jimmy Tock (the main character) is revealed as the villain's fraternal twin brother.<br /> <br />But wait, there's even more! When that ending didn't pack enough of a punch for Koontz's liking, we find out that Jimmy and the villain are really the product of an incestuous relationship between their biological grandfather/father and his daughter/their mother. <br /> <br />And if you place an order in the next thirty seconds, we'll throw in lots more! Four chapters from the end, Jimmy's first-person POV switches to the wife's first-person POV so that Koontz can attempt to trick us into believing that Jimmy dies. But Jimmy doesn't die. What a hoot, huh? A real rib-tickler of a twist!<br /> <br />To quote the book:<br /> <br />"Okay, we yanked your chain again, like we did back in chapter twenty-four. How much fun would it have been, there in the big top, if you'd been absolutely certain that I had survived?"<br /> <br />Are you joshing me? Pulling E.'s leg, perhaps? Legitimately, you're going to break the fourth wall to say that shit? I should punch you in your cocksucker with a hot-water bottle full of nitroglycerin. It wasn't funny. It sure as shit wasn't cute. It was pretentious. Pure, lazy-ass, unoriginal, amateur bullshit. You, your editor, your publisher, and anyone who recommends this book to a human being who isn't their most reviled enemy should have to read this book over and over and over again, forever and ever, amen. <br /> <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="3715713450389731876" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2830301730335661914">"Some lives, conducted with grace, are beautiful arcs bridging this world to eternity."<br/><br/>"No one's life should be rooted in fear. We are born for wonder, for joy, for hope, for love, to marvel at the mystery of existence, to be ravished by the beauty of the world, to seek truth and meaning, to acquire wisdom, and by our treatment of others to brighten the corner where we are."<br/><br/>"No one can grant you happiness. Happiness is a choice we all have the power to make."<br/><br/>Ever since first reading this book 20</span>
  <span id="freeText2830301730335661914" style="display:none">"Some lives, conducted with grace, are beautiful arcs bridging this world to eternity."<br /><br />"No one's life should be rooted in fear. We are born for wonder, for joy, for hope, for love, to marvel at the mystery of existence, to be ravished by the beauty of the world, to seek truth and meaning, to acquire wisdom, and by our treatment of others to brighten the corner where we are."<br /><br />"No one can grant you happiness. Happiness is a choice we all have the power to make."<br /><br />Ever since first reading this book 2005, I have considered it my very favorite Dean Koontz novel. After reading it for the fifth time, this masterpiece retains it's rank of my #1 favorite :-) I will admit it has some fierce competition with "By the Light of the Moon", "Relentless" and "Cold Fire", but it stands as a shining star of my favorites for it's superb mix of simplicity, humor, suspense and twists. It's lessons on life and family are wonderful - the clowns and aerialists are entertaining :-) and we mustn't forget Grandma Roweena (my favorite character in the story) <br /><br />PREPARE TO BE ENCHANTED!!!!!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2830301730335661914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_61290502" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61290502" />
    <a title="Remy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/984541-remy"><img alt="Remy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1205603791p2/984541.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61290502?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Remy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Remy" href="/user/show/984541-remy">Remy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61290502" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13373975663499350776">This is my first Dean Koontz book. I expected a horror story, but this book was actually quite funny. I just grabbed it off the paperback section of the library to take to the beach, and I was pleasantly surprised. Initially, I found the main character, Jimmy, a little like Ignatiuos Reilly from "Confederacy of Dunces," but he actually had a great deal more sense. I think it was more in Koontz's writing style. The characters were so likeable, that I wanted to be friends with them; except for the</span>
  <span id="freeText13373975663499350776" style="display:none">This is my first Dean Koontz book. I expected a horror story, but this book was actually quite funny. I just grabbed it off the paperback section of the library to take to the beach, and I was pleasantly surprised. Initially, I found the main character, Jimmy, a little like Ignatiuos Reilly from "Confederacy of Dunces," but he actually had a great deal more sense. I think it was more in Koontz's writing style. The characters were so likeable, that I wanted to be friends with them; except for the clowns. Yes, there were clowns, and while this was weird and bizarre, I somehow sort of got what Koontz was trying to do. This book is eccentric because it combines the bizarro with the little sweet lessons about the good and honest things in life- love, family, and faith. I found myself rereading some of the poignant passages that Koontz would slip into this wacko story because they were so well written and so meaningful. I doubt many people will feel the same way about this book as I did, but to anyone who reads it, I recommend that you lighten up and not take things too seriously when you read it. There are unexpected surprises and alot of humor. This is always a good mix.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13373975663499350776" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

At times, I was so anxious to know how a particular situation would turn out that I'd find myself skipping ahead a couple of chapters, and then immediately scoldi</span>
  <span id="freeText5839243118527554737" style="display:none">Wow! This book totally blew me away. Best Koontz book I've read yet, and I thought Odd Thomas would be a tough act to follow. I may have to pick up a copy of this book for own PC bookshelf so I can let hubby and all my other non-BC friends read it too. :)<br /><br />Great character development, suspense, the kind of book I didn't want to put down. At times, I was so anxious to know how a particular situation would turn out that I'd find myself skipping ahead a couple of chapters, and then immediately scolding myself for doing so. Screw going to bed! I <i>must</i> read a few more chapters so I can see how this part turns out! LOL <br /><br />Though it contained plenty of suspense, it was also a love story in a sense, not a romance, but so many parts of the book, and particularly the end, gave me the warm fuzzies--the kind that bring tears to your eyes. I guess touching is the right word, as the story touched me on so many levels. It made me laugh, it made me cry, it held me in thrall. An excellent read indeed!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5839243118527554737" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer8618319583480961923">I read this book for the first time in August or September of 2011, which started my love of Dean Koontz novels. Now, five years later, I've finally read this book again. And it's just as enchanting as I remember. <br/><br/>The events of this novel don't seem like they would make a compelling story, and yet they do.   <span id="freeText8618319583480961923" style="display:none">I read this book for the first time in August or September of 2011, which started my love of Dean Koontz novels. Now, five years later, I've finally read this book again. And it's just as enchanting as I remember. <br /><br />The events of this novel don't seem like they would make a compelling story, and yet they do. It's filled with a cast of varied and realistic characters, the struggle between good and evil, and the message that there is always a reason to hope. <br /><br />Koontz has a beautiful style that has me laughing out loud often, even when the characters are facing life and death situations. There is horror in the novel, just like there is in the world. And if there is horror then should there not also be humor? <br /><br />I adore the tale of Jimmy, Lorrie, Annie, Lucy, Andy, Rudy, Maddy, and Rowena. Such a great family. And the ending was perfect.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10087139631237491807">3 ½ stars. Tongue in cheek mystery suspense.<br/><br/>I was disappointed when I saw this was first person. Sometimes it works, but usually it’s not as good for me, and in this case first person did not help. The plot premise was odd. It didn’t draw me in as much as other plots. When the book begins, we are told something disastrous will happen five times during Jimmy’s life. It feels like a scavenger hunt. The first day comes and we watch a disaster which Jimmy survives. Then we wait a little and watch th</span>
  <span id="freeText10087139631237491807" style="display:none">3 ½ stars. Tongue in cheek mystery suspense.<br /><br />I was disappointed when I saw this was first person. Sometimes it works, but usually it’s not as good for me, and in this case first person did not help. The plot premise was odd. It didn’t draw me in as much as other plots. When the book begins, we are told something disastrous will happen five times during Jimmy’s life. It feels like a scavenger hunt. The first day comes and we watch a disaster which Jimmy survives. Then we wait a little and watch the second disaster. I did not enjoy the formula. It felt staged - contrived. It was hard to identify with.<br /><br />BUT, because the author is such a talented writer, there were many parts I enjoyed. I laughed or chuckled several times - especially at Lorrie’s comments. If you’re new to this author, don’t start with this book. See below for my favorites.<br /><br />The narrator John Bedford Lloyd was great. I was pleased that he used a soft, feminine voice for Lorrie without sounding like an effeminate gay guy.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ There was one illogical and stupid part. On one of the disaster days, a box is delivered to Jimmy at his home. He hopes it is not a bomb. So why does he open the box in the presence of his loved ones? He should have had the police bomb squad x-ray it and do it for him.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />DATA:<br />Narrative mode: first person Jimmy and a little Lorrie. Unabridged audiobook reading time: 11 hrs and 30 mins. Swearing language: (rarely used) moderate. Sexual content: none. Setting: 1974 to 2005 Colorado. Book copyright: 2004. Genre: mystery suspense. Ending: happy.<br /><br />OTHER BOOKS:<br />I’ve reviewed the following Dean Koontz books. Dates are copyright dates.<br /><br />5 stars. Watchers 1987<br />5 stars. Lightning 1988<br />5 stars. Intensity 1995<br />3 stars. Odd Thomas 2003<br />3 ½ stars. Life Expectancy 2004<br />2 stars. Velocity 2005</span>

<div id="review_413109850" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/413109850" />
    <a title="Christopher Owen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2843537-christopher-owen"><img alt="Christopher Owen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442633656p2/2843537.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/413109850?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher Owen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher Owen" href="/user/show/2843537-christopher-owen">Christopher Owen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer413109850" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6364461193948611790">Simply put, this novel is nothing short of incredible. Highly original, darkly amusing and almost (ALMOST) too clever for it's own good. <br/><br/>For me the mark of a good book is the inability to put it down. Getting so caught up in the story and the characters that you think abouto it even when you're not reading it. <br/><br/>I'll try to avoid spoilers, but my favorite thing about this novel is how evrerything is connected. It's obvious that Koontz had every detail planned in advance. One thing leads to another</span>
  <span id="freeText6364461193948611790" style="display:none">Simply put, this novel is nothing short of incredible. Highly original, darkly amusing and almost (ALMOST) too clever for it's own good. <br /><br />For me the mark of a good book is the inability to put it down. Getting so caught up in the story and the characters that you think abouto it even when you're not reading it. <br /><br />I'll try to avoid spoilers, but my favorite thing about this novel is how evrerything is connected. It's obvious that Koontz had every detail planned in advance. One thing leads to another, that leads to something else and so on and so forth until it finally comes full circle. I'll still be thinking about this book in the weeks to come, trying to work out what kind of 'lesson' (so to speak) I got from the story. While it's vastly importment for a story to be entertaining it also helps greatly when the reader can take something out from the book. When the reader is touched emotionally and learns just a miniscule fact concerning this crazy thing we call life, that's when we have something special. <br /><br />Off the top of my head I can cite hope as a theme. Hope in the face of tragedy and misery and still being brave enough to hope for the best. When Jimmy and Laurie are <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ discussing cynicisim versus pessicisim I think it's a bit of a turning point for the two of them. As they become a married couple I think they balance eachother out nicely, allowing them to survive the five horrible days which were prophecized with grace and dignity. To finish, there's also a line towards the end citing the opposite of hope is desperation. I feel that that fits quite nicely.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="6364461193948611790" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1391474" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1391474" />
    <a title="Jack" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/87898-jack"><img alt="Jack" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298555795p2/87898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1391474?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jack" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jack" href="/user/show/87898-jack">Jack</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">only bored die-hard Koontz fans </span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jack by: </span>
      a crazed clownish friend
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1391474" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7280715119253548073"> Dean Koontz reigns as the Henry Ford of novelists. Ford invented assembly line production, a way of mass-producing automobiles. Ford out produced other automakers and at the same time delivered a quality product. His Model T remains perhaps the most successful automobile ever made. Ford Motor Company occasionally made mistakes; the Edsel of the mid-1950s was its most glaring and infamous. Dean Koontz, like Ford has developed a method of cranking out novels at an amazing pace, over 60 in the spa</span>
  <span id="freeText7280715119253548073" style="display:none"> Dean Koontz reigns as the Henry Ford of novelists. Ford invented assembly line production, a way of mass-producing automobiles. Ford out produced other automakers and at the same time delivered a quality product. His Model T remains perhaps the most successful automobile ever made. Ford Motor Company occasionally made mistakes; the Edsel of the mid-1950s was its most glaring and infamous. Dean Koontz, like Ford has developed a method of cranking out novels at an amazing pace, over 60 in the span of about 30 years. Koontz too, has developed a wildly popular product: 22 New York Times #1 bestsellers, and dozens more making the bestseller list. He also makes mistakes, and Life Expectancy rattletraps along as Dean Koontz’s Edsel. <br /><br />Koontz's standard assembly line formula calls for immediately tossing the reader into an improbable situation, usually involving something supernatural that stretches belief. As his story rolls down the line, Koontz, through strong, believable characters and meticulous research, constructs plausibility. Once his readers take the hook, Koontz leads them on a Ludlum-like roller coaster ride culminating in a rip-roaring climax. The finished product routinely sells, and Life Expectancy, another bestseller, illustrates that. But, despite Koontz’s success and book sales, Life satisfies like years-old McDonald’s fries lifted from the floorboard of an old Edsel.<br /> <br />True to formula, Koontz starts "Life" with a supernatural bang: “On the night that I was born, my paternal grandfather, Josef Tock, made ten predictions that shaped my life. Then he died in the very minute that my mother gave birth to me." It seems our narrating hero Jimmy Tock’s grandpa, while in his hospital death bed, predicts ten days of life threatening – life changing crises for about to be born Jimmy’s future, down to the very year, date and even day of the week they will arrive. Fittingly, this night of Jimmy’s birth and of his grandpa’s date-picking looms as an ominously dark and stormy one. Jimmy’s dad Rudy Tock dutifully writes down each dreaded date on a circus pass that Konrad Beezo, a manifestly insane circus clown that Rudy met in the waiting room, gave him. At the same time, Beezo is being informed by a doctor that his wife, a circus aerialist, has just died giving birth to a baby boy. Beezo, being insane and not too happy about the news, responds in a very unclown-like manner, gunning down everyone: doctors, nurses, patients, cops, but spares Rudy, his wife and their just-born little Jimmy because Rudy, after all, had become his waiting room buddy. Beezo then grabs his own newborn son and flees into the stormy night. Typical of Koontz’s formula, this opening sounds improbable and stretches belief. But we ain't seen nothin' yet.<br /> <br /> At this point Koontz usually displays his skillful ability to build complex, likeable or hate-able characters that we really love or loathe. He dazzles us with special knowledge on the intricacies of his premise. Not so this time. Jimmy, now grown and following in chef dad’s footsteps, becomes an apprentice pastry chef. But more than fluffy crullers, Jimmy cooks comedy. If Comedy Central Network had a cooking show, Jimmy would be a natural. And apparently its genetic, because Mom, Dad and Grandma Rowena appear as stand-up-comic wannabes, which Koontz artificially wields, brandishing some witty dinner table conversations.<br /> <br /> Along about the fifth prediction (the predictions tend to run together after awhile) 20 year-old Jimmy meets his wife-to-be, Lorrie. They bump into each other while being held hostage by an insane criminal who happens to be setting explosives in the old mine tunnels underneath their Colorado town. The criminal determines to destroy the entire town just after he robs the bank. This evil doer’s name? Why it’s Punchinello Beezo, the insane clown Konrad’s now grown son, who Jimmy snidely describes as “a graduate of Hannibal Lecter University, ready for a career in hospitality services as the new manager of the Bates motel." Punchinello’s accomplices are Honker and Crinkles…yes, two circus clowns. Lorrie, as coincidence would have it, also happens to be a natural born comedienne. Jimmy and Lorrie fall in love while entertaining Punchinello and his clown henchmen with impromptu comedy routines to relieve the tension as the villains set explosives. The evil triumvirate serves as a Bud Abbott for the lovebirds Lou Costello while they prepare to destroy the town.<br /><br /> Forget Koontz’s formula,these characters and this tale crossover into preposterous. "Life’s" premise and its characters ring patently false, functioning as mere vehicles to steer Koontz’s brand-new comedic engine. For Koontz fans, Life drifts so far from his natural course, we are left clutching for the wheel and scratching our heads. Is it camp, or is it crap? It’s almost as if Koontz has been waiting his whole life to write this book, as if Brando was crying out: “I coulda been a comedy writer! I coulda been a comedy writer, instead of a novelist, which is what I am.” Yet Koontz didn’t peddle however many millions of books by being a poor writer. In its way, Life entertains, and there exists strewn about, moments of excellent craft, like the smattering of lifeboats rowing away from the Titanic.<br /><br /> In some ironic sense, Jimmy Tock’s book-ending reflection on life’s meaning also mirrors Life’s epitaph. “By laughing, you do not use up your laughter, but increase your store of it” Jimmy preaches. And Koontz does provide a packed carload of clown’s humor, but too often we find ourselves laughing at the author and his buffoonish characters. Jimmy further instructs: “the more you expect from life, the more your expectations will be fulfilled.” We expected more from Koontz. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7280715119253548073" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_124380787" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124380787" />
    <a title="Johnny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3784000-johnny"><img alt="Johnny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545250646p2/3784000.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/124380787?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Johnny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Johnny" href="/user/show/3784000-johnny">Johnny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789024551750" href="/book/show/9642098-tijd-van-leven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3784000-johnny?shelf=own-it">own-it</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer124380787&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer124380787" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5903878712931933608">If there is any Koontz novel I wish I'd written myself, it's "Life Expectancy”. This book hits all the right chords: the absurdity of life, the interconnectedness of human beings, recognizable characters, signature humor, all put together with the reality of sudden violence.<br/>	<br/>Minutes before Jimmy Tock was born, his dying grandfather made several predictions not only about his birth, but also gave warnings of five terrible days to come in his future. Together with his family, Jimmy prepares himsel</span>
  <span id="freeText5903878712931933608" style="display:none">If there is any Koontz novel I wish I'd written myself, it's "Life Expectancy”. This book hits all the right chords: the absurdity of life, the interconnectedness of human beings, recognizable characters, signature humor, all put together with the reality of sudden violence.<br />	<br />Minutes before Jimmy Tock was born, his dying grandfather made several predictions not only about his birth, but also gave warnings of five terrible days to come in his future. Together with his family, Jimmy prepares himself for the inevitable.<br />	<br />Told from Jimmy’s own first person perspective – and partly from his wife’s at the end of the novel, in a kind of cheesy “gotcha there!” attempt to add a rather fabricated final twist – the book wraps you in its arms of familiarity. This technique, previously applied to novels as “Twilight Eyes”, the Chris Snow books and the Odd Thomas series, is one of Koontz’s greatest accomplishments as a writer. The only downside of it in this particular case, might be the load of bakery and pastry analogies Jimmy Tock often resorts to while describing something.<br />	<br />Spending time at the Tock family dinner table is like a verbal rollercoaster ride – only backwards; you don’t see the loops coming until you’re suddenly upside down already. Grandma Rowena herself alone is worth her weight in gold.<br />	<br />The first of the terrible days Jimmy has to face is excellent storytelling, moreso because it’s the most incredible fashion in which the hero and heroine in a Koontz tale meet up.<br />	<br />From the moment Lorrie Lynn Hicks walked through that library door, I shared Jimmy’s sentiments and fell head over heels in love with her. Like Jimmy says, she’s the type of girl you want your family to meet. She’s so ad lib, imaginative, resourceful and brave, but also vulnerable, scared and insecure at the times she should be.<br />	<br />While riddled with violent events, “Life Expectancy” is a story filled with humor, which displays itself mostly through the dialogues. These characters often go on wild tangents, make crazy out-of-place remarks, create misunderstandings, and all the while there’s simply no telling whether they’re serious about it or just pulling someone’s leg.<br />	<br />Some of the more action driven parts, like the off-road driving in a snowstorm, can become a little boring after a while, but only compared to the other scenes where heroes and villains trade wisecracks. The lack of witty dialogue is only natural in this case, but it creates the risk of the story getting a bit dumbed down to nothing but a cheap action tale.<br />	<br />The later chapters trade in some of the humor for some heavy drama. When Lorrie gets shot and has to be rushed to the hospital, we feel with Jimmy and totally understand his sentiments when he says that “we don't fear our own deaths as much as the deaths of those we love”. We remember from “Odd Thomas” that Koontz doesn’t always necessarily write a happy ending for his characters – and at the ending of “Life Expectancy” we see that he even tries to copy his misleading about Stormy Llewellyn’s death in a rather unsuccesful practical joke.<br />	<br />The most devastating chapters, however, must be when the story’s real twist is revealed, Jimmy turns out to be a Beezo biologically, and the villain is the only suitable donor for an ill child. Punchinello’s attitude in the prison, the reaction of both Jimmy and Lorrie, and his sudden change of heart seem to be ripped out of some soap opera – not to mention the baby switching at birth – but it never comes over as an old cliché; it works and fits the rest of the book.<br />	<br />This novel is actually unique in that it has so many villains, the better to illustrate the differences between good and evil. Not only are there father and son Beezo, but also grandpa Vivacemente who makes for a very formidable final nemesis.<br />	<br />“Life Expectancy” is about man being confronted with his own mortality. Because you have no idea what the future holds in store for you, you’d better enjoy life to the fullest, embrace your family and friends, choose the path that destiny lays out for you. Our lives aren’t shaped solely by the environment in which we grow up, but also by our own personal decisions.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5903878712931933608" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_5703593" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5703593" />
    <a title="William Dalphin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/258706-william-dalphin"><img alt="William Dalphin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505763680p2/258706.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/5703593?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William Dalphin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William Dalphin" href="/user/show/258706-william-dalphin">William Dalphin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5703593" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer337558858907978428">I am going to use my review of Dean Koontz's <i>Life Expectancy</i> to discuss a matter that seems of great importance in most of Koontz's work, mental illness. Koontz often simplifies his antagonists' motivations to the basic idea, "This person is insane." "Why is that?" you may ask. Well, I'll tell you. It's because labeling a character as insane means that Koontz can throw logic and rationality right out the window. Why does this person kill entire families, collecting trophies of his murders? Becau</span>
  <span id="freeText337558858907978428" style="display:none">I am going to use my review of Dean Koontz's <i>Life Expectancy</i> to discuss a matter that seems of great importance in most of Koontz's work, mental illness. Koontz often simplifies his antagonists' motivations to the basic idea, "This person is insane." "Why is that?" you may ask. Well, I'll tell you. It's because labeling a character as insane means that Koontz can throw logic and rationality right out the window. Why does this person kill entire families, collecting trophies of his murders? Because this person is insane. Why does that person stalk and secretly harass a random individual, nailing his hand to the floor and framing him for a murder? Because that person is insane. It's an easy (and lazy) fallback that Koontz relies on again and again to propel his plots forward.<br /><br />More baffling than his dedication to making everyone on the wrong side of law and order insane, is Koontz's strange belief that insane people all share a single inherent quality beyond simply doing crazy shit to throw everything around them into chaos: they all believe that eating something gives you its power. Cue the braking and stunned silence. "What is that?" you say, "Do you have evidence about this?" Oh, follow along, dear reader.<br /><br />Example 1: In Dean Koontz's <i>Intensity</i>, the crazed killer, Edgar Vess, takes a moment from his swift and silent murder spree through the Templeton household to casually consume a small spider. We are then granted a brief insight into the killer's brain as he feels the spider's power flow through him.<br /><br />Example 2: In Dean Koontz's <i>Forever Odd</i>, the prime antagonist, Datura, the evil phone sex operator (where does he come up with this shit?), plans to cut out and consume Odd's heart so as to gain his ability to see the dead.<br /><br />Example 3: <i>Life Expectancy</i>, which you are here to read about and I will now briefly discuss, has a scene where Punchinello Beezo, the insane clown (see comment about evil phone sex operator above) stops to eat a spider. Now, if you've read the book, you will say, "Aha! But there's nothing in the book that says that Punchinello thinks he's gaining the spider's power!" Yes, that's true. But given Koontz's track record for crazy people eating things and thinking they've gained the power of it, I think it's safe to say that Punchinello believed he had spider essence running through his veins just before he lost his ability to have kids.<br /><br />Now, I've not read all of Koontz's work, and there are a few stories of his that I've read where the crazy person does NOT eat anything living. But then, in most cases, the antagonist's actions and thoughts are usually kept hidden, so for all we know, they were busy gobbling up a bowl of Spider-O's before making their way to the mall to shoot up everybody in sight.<br /><br />Anyway, with that off my chest, <i>Life Expectancy</i> is Dean Koontz's means to argue the case of nurture over nature using precognition, a mentally unstable circus troupe, a family of bakers, one unfortunate spider and a series of seemingly implausible and head-scratchingly stupid events. It is an argument he ultimately fails simply because it ignores any sort of reasoning or explanation in favor of "these people are all two screws short of a two screw bucket". I personally think it works better as another example of Dean Koontz's deep, deep hatred for the rest of humanity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="337558858907978428" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_11303461" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11303461" />
    <a title="Myr" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/419239-myr"><img alt="Myr" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598792428p2/419239.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/11303461?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Myr" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Myr" href="/user/show/419239-myr">Myr</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Koontz fans, fans of thrillers with a dash of the supernatural</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419239-myr?shelf=finished">finished</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer11303461" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2804099659656840017">This one took me a couple weeks to read, but I did finish it (good sign #1) and by the time I was done, I didn't feel like I'd wasted my time (good sign #2).<br/><br/>It's written in the first person, which works better here than in Koontz's <u>The Taking</u>, but not as well as it did in <u>Odd Thomas</u>. The premise of the story is a fun one: on the night of Jimmy Tock's birth, his dying grandfather predicts the time of Jimmy's birth, his weight, a physical ailment...and that Jimmy will suffer five terrible days in </span>
  <span id="freeText2804099659656840017" style="display:none">This one took me a couple weeks to read, but I did finish it (good sign #1) and by the time I was done, I didn't feel like I'd wasted my time (good sign #2).<br /><br />It's written in the first person, which works better here than in Koontz's <u>The Taking</u>, but not as well as it did in <u>Odd Thomas</u>. The premise of the story is a fun one: on the night of Jimmy Tock's birth, his dying grandfather predicts the time of Jimmy's birth, his weight, a physical ailment...and that Jimmy will suffer five terrible days in his life. Considerately, Jimmy's grandfather even offers the dates of these five terrible days.<br /><br />Koontz loves his quirky characters, and Jimmy - a baker in a small Colorado ski town - is no exception. He, his family, and the love of his life all share a healthy, if somewhat off-kilter sense of humor, and that sense of humor is displayed throughout the book, despite the threat of the Five Terrible Days that haunt Jimmy's life. I enjoyed Jimmy's family, although sometimes, their dialogue tries too hard for witty and it shows. <br /><br />The base premise of this one totally carries the story; I really wanted to know what each of the five days would bring. It's a classic Koontz story of Love/Good v. Madness/Evil, with just enough of a supernatural underpinning to keep it from being an overly morbid, could have been ripped from the headlines kind of read.<br /><br />Plot twists abound in the last third of the book, but thankfully, they all feel pretty organic. <u>Life Expectancy</u>'s not a re-reader for me, but I liked it well enough to recommend.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2804099659656840017" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17841993181922441463">I’ve inherited plenty of things from my mother (looks, laugh, sense of humor, mannerisms), and among those is a predilection for mystery novels, Dean Koontz, and plot twists. This book contains all three of these things. <br/><br/>If you’re craving a book with action that starts right away, and a plot that surprises you at every page-turn, this is a book for you. <br/><br/>If you enjoy relatable, humorous characters that provide a personable narration, this is a book for you. <br/><br/>If you like short chapters (another thi</span>
  <span id="freeText17841993181922441463" style="display:none">I’ve inherited plenty of things from my mother (looks, laugh, sense of humor, mannerisms), and among those is a predilection for mystery novels, Dean Koontz, and plot twists. This book contains all three of these things. <br /><br />If you’re craving a book with action that starts right away, and a plot that surprises you at every page-turn, this is a book for you. <br /><br />If you enjoy relatable, humorous characters that provide a personable narration, this is a book for you. <br /><br />If you like short chapters (another thing I got from my mom) that make you feel like a super-sonic fast reader, this is a book for you. <br /><br />If you enjoy mysteries and puzzles, this is a book for you. <br /><br />If you want an easy-reading book to have in your spare time, but still want to be entertained (and this book provides knock-your-socks-off entertainment), this is a book for you. <br /><br />If you hate clowns, well, you still will like this book but it definitely won’t help your suspicion of creepy circus clowns (the clown thing isn’t pervasive or haunting, don’t worry). <br /><br />If you’re gluten-free, this is STILL a book for you even though the main character is a bread maker. Yes, that’s right, clowns AND bakers. Don’t forget about those plot twists I was talking about. <br /><br />My mom didn’t skimp on giving me good-book-detecting genes. This is a great read and easily one of my favorite Koontz novels. It’s perfect for a weekend or vacation read. It’s like a roller coaster: it may leave you dazed, but you’ll be so satisfied by the end. <br /><br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14262770550414329724">Jason gave me this book for Christmas - he had listened to it as book on tape and really liked it, and thought I might like it too. I didn't - I LOVED it!! Laugh out loud funny at times, and yet also reflects on what makes a good life, relationship, and family, all in a suspenseful story!<br/><br/>I loved the characters - the main one, Jimmy Tock, but also all the supporting cast of characters. Each one was fleshed out and real. There was a unique banter amongst his family that I both recognized my own fa</span>
  <span id="freeText14262770550414329724" style="display:none">Jason gave me this book for Christmas - he had listened to it as book on tape and really liked it, and thought I might like it too. I didn't - I LOVED it!! Laugh out loud funny at times, and yet also reflects on what makes a good life, relationship, and family, all in a suspenseful story!<br /><br />I loved the characters - the main one, Jimmy Tock, but also all the supporting cast of characters. Each one was fleshed out and real. There was a unique banter amongst his family that I both recognized my own family in, and also wanted to be even more like. The story itself was engaging - I'm not sure how I'd really classify it, it had elements of drama, mystery, suspense, humor, and even romance (not gross fake romance, but real, sarcastic, honest, loving, inspiring romance). <br /><br />The book subtly reflects on themes such as living your life to the fullest despite not knowing what the future holds, what makes "family", good vs. evil, the meaning of life.... all wrapped up in a story with bakers, evil clowns, crazy people, car chases and twists and turns that caught me off guard throughout.<br /><br />This was a fast read, both because it's so well written, and also because I was so dying to know how it happened that I picked it and didn't put it down until I got through it!.<br /><br />Inspiring, entertaining, well written - I highly recommend to anyone! :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="14262770550414329724" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/137273-eh-eh?shelf=babble-added">babble-added</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31240084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16970552519698080764">I tend to avoid authors with huge shelves of published work (Danielle Steele, Piers Anthony, etc.) because I tried and found them incredibly unreadable. Mr. DK isn't bad, though - very entertaining, humor, twists.<br/><br/>This story centered around a young man who, at his birth, had ominous predictions of 5 horrendous (or was it terrible?) days in his future. He got through it with family support, practicality, blahblahblah. The circus is involved. And there's implied sex crime. The twist at the end seem</span>
  <span id="freeText16970552519698080764" style="display:none">I tend to avoid authors with huge shelves of published work (Danielle Steele, Piers Anthony, etc.) because I tried and found them incredibly unreadable. Mr. DK isn't bad, though - very entertaining, humor, twists.<br /><br />This story centered around a young man who, at his birth, had ominous predictions of 5 horrendous (or was it terrible?) days in his future. He got through it with family support, practicality, blahblahblah. The circus is involved. And there's implied sex crime. The twist at the end seemed rushed, like DK was tired of writing and wanted to wrap up in a hurry. Not that it was too much of a surprise.<br /><br />My biggest and most petty complaint is that he uses Dan Brown paragraphs, i.e., nearly every sentence. Do they think the reader doesn't have the attention span to keep up with a single thought through multiple sentences. I find that insulting. Insulting! I guess it's a trick to make your book seem longer. I used to do that in elementary school, where I got to the end of that 2 page essay by writing large/using 12.5 or 13pt font (don't want to be too obvious by using 14pt). Maybe Mr. DK is trying to expand his reader base by identifying with the average elementary schoolchild? Argh.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16970552519698080764" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="Amy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5473750-amy"><img alt="Amy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1305866115p2/5473750.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/302104392?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy" href="/user/show/5473750-amy">Amy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer302104392" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13947529057433301682">Jimmy Tock is probably my favorite of all of Dean Koontz characters - and that's a lot considering one of Koontz' strengths is character development. Jimmy was born on the day his grandfather died. Before his grandfather died and the minute of Jimmy's birth, his grandfather sat up in bed and stated that Jimmy would have five horrible days. He listed the dates in question. Jimmy's family didn't know what to make of Jimmy's grandfather's prophesy until the first of the dates arrived and Jimmy, ind</span>
  <span id="freeText13947529057433301682" style="display:none">Jimmy Tock is probably my favorite of all of Dean Koontz characters - and that's a lot considering one of Koontz' strengths is character development. Jimmy was born on the day his grandfather died. Before his grandfather died and the minute of Jimmy's birth, his grandfather sat up in bed and stated that Jimmy would have five horrible days. He listed the dates in question. Jimmy's family didn't know what to make of Jimmy's grandfather's prophesy until the first of the dates arrived and Jimmy, indeed, had a horrible day, filled with tragedy. The second day on the list was the same. The book really starts on the third of Jimmy's horrible days and the events that result.<br /><br />Jimmy is such a normal, lovable character, that you'll find yourself rooting for him more than most others. He's a normal baker; a good, honest man who works hard and always tries to do what's right. He has a wonderful family who raised him well and later a family of his own that he raises the same way. But there are three more horrible days to get through.....after the first two, you start to wonder if he can survive them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13947529057433301682" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="The Face of Your Father" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/79119566-the-face-of-your-father"><img alt="The Face of Your Father" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597595315p2/79119566.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2490334207?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="The Face of Your Father" class="user" itemprop="url" name="The Face of Your Father" href="/user/show/79119566-the-face-of-your-father">The Face of Your Father</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2490334207" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12354969898694807213">“Prepare to be enchanted.”<br/><br/>Upon the birth of Jimmy Tock, his dying grandfather made five predictions for the arriving infant. Five terrible days will occur between his twentieth and thirtieth years of life. What lies ahead for the Tock family as doomsday approaches on the calendar? <br/><br/>‘Life Expectancy’ is a tongue-in-cheek mystery suspense story that possesses the qualities of absurdist comedy. It shouldn’t work, but it does.<br/><br/>It is a dazzling read. Koontz has rarely been whimsical in my opinion, yet </span>
  <span id="freeText12354969898694807213" style="display:none">“Prepare to be enchanted.”<br /><br />Upon the birth of Jimmy Tock, his dying grandfather made five predictions for the arriving infant. Five terrible days will occur between his twentieth and thirtieth years of life. What lies ahead for the Tock family as doomsday approaches on the calendar? <br /><br />‘Life Expectancy’ is a tongue-in-cheek mystery suspense story that possesses the qualities of absurdist comedy. It shouldn’t work, but it does.<br /><br />It is a dazzling read. Koontz has rarely been whimsical in my opinion, yet ‘Life Expectancy’ displays a magical aura that surrounds it from its opening pages. Koontz displays a mostly unseen talent of humor within this work as a chuckle can be discovered in nearly every chapter. An author mostly associated with the supernatural tales of horror unexpectedly delivers a tale of love, family, fate and delicious baked goods. <br /><br />When Koontz travels beyond his “comfort zone” with novels like “Icebound”, “The Husband”, and <br />“Life Expectancy”, there are not many who can challenge him in terms of imagination and enthusiasm. Despite the lack of anything supernatural and the overall absurdity of them as characters, Koontz creates genuinely unsettling antagonists who nearly steal this story from the lovely Tock protagonists who might just be Koontz’s most charming creation. <br /><br />Homicidal clowns, castration, bank robberies, a demented circus, French baked goods, fart humor, syndactyly, doomsday preparations, incest, and predictions for the future. Underneath it all, the love and power of family. 5/5</span>
  <a data-text-id="12354969898694807213" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3058030323" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3058030323" />
    <a title="Saniya Ahmad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14907129-saniya-ahmad"><img alt="Saniya Ahmad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594197178p2/14907129.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3058030323?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Saniya Ahmad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Saniya Ahmad" href="/user/show/14907129-saniya-ahmad">Saniya Ahmad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007196951" href="/book/show/959353.Life_Expectancy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3058030323" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13510139119504859821">Life Expectancy by Dean Koontz is a book about Jimmy Tock who enters this world as his grandfather is leaving it. His grandfather,l Josef Tock, in his dying moments, has declared and named 5 days that will be extremely dark and terrible for Jimmy. With the end of this declaration, he hiccups and dies.<br/><br/>Rudy, Jimmy's father, believes this is utter crap. But minutes before this prediction, Josef had also predicted that his grandson will be called Jimmy (something he could not know since the gender o</span>
  <span id="freeText13510139119504859821" style="display:none">Life Expectancy by Dean Koontz is a book about Jimmy Tock who enters this world as his grandfather is leaving it. His grandfather,l Josef Tock, in his dying moments, has declared and named 5 days that will be extremely dark and terrible for Jimmy. With the end of this declaration, he hiccups and dies.<br /><br />Rudy, Jimmy's father, believes this is utter crap. But minutes before this prediction, Josef had also predicted that his grandson will be called Jimmy (something he could not know since the gender of the baby had not been defined during the pregnancy) and he will be born at 10.46 PM, with syndactyly, which was exactly what had happened. Rudy now fears what will happen to his son on the 5 mentioned days that made his own father whimper and tremble at the horror.<br /><br />The first date to occur is when Jimmy is 20 years old. He has been brought up by his loving parents to face this day. But what nightmares will he face? Will he be forced to lose someone or something he truly loves? Or will he be forced to make choices that he doesn't want to? Each day brings its own challenges but also shapes who Jimmy Tock is and who he is supposed to be.<br /><br />A few people recommended this book when I asked for suggestions for a light, funny read. It has a very hilarious humour style which at times feel utterly stupid and dumb and therefore does make you laugh out loud. However, it got really old really fast.<br /><br />This book was kind of a chore to finish. I did not like the plot story too much, I was hoping it would be more interesting that it was; halfway through I kept falling asleep whenever I picked up the book. It got too monotonous some times; at others it seemed that the author was trying too hard.<br /><br />Some parts of the book were definitely enjoyable and my favourite line from the book has to be "After the pain passes, there is always cake". But overall, the book was tremendously underwhelming and I can safely say that in terms of books, this year has started terribly. 🙈<br /><br />I sincerely apologize to all Dean Koontz fans but this book was not for me.<br /><br />2/5</span>
  <a data-text-id="13510139119504859821" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('q/GHFhvbJHL6TmyOF210x0k/Oca1p4CJFICLE2JR7gY8v5JDogtr6IHlbVd4Z1gQM4YMZ8azjajX/iBWeiivKw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('HnSWkucq7GOyLIAdxGvTxHTv8/QD/5O77dIucmtPlnKJOoPHXvqj+cmHgcSrYf8TDlbGVXDrnpourIU3czbXXw==')})
  }
//]]>

Almost from the beginning, its grandeur has been scarred by episodes of madness, suicide, mass murder, and whispers of things far worse. But since its rechristening in the 1970s as a luxury apartment building, the Pendleton has been at peace. For its fortunate residents—among them a successful songwriter and her young son, a disgraced ex-senator, a widowed attorney, and a driven money manager—the Pendleton’s magnificent quarters are a sanctuary, its dark past all but forgotten.\n\nBut now inexplicable shadows caper across walls, security cameras relay impossible images, phantom voices mutter in strange tongues, not-quite-human figures lurk in the basement, elevators plunge into unknown depths. With each passing hour, a terrifying certainty grows: Whatever drove the Pendleton’s past occupants to their unspeakable fates is at work again.   <span id="freeText4475428847834771921" style="display:none">Acknowledged as "America's most popular suspense novelist" (Rolling Stone) and as one of today's most celebrated and successful writers, Dean Ray Koontz has earned the devotion of millions of readers around the world and the praise of critics everywhere for tales of character, mystery, and adventure that strike to the core of what it means to be human.<br /><br />Dean, the author of many #1 New York Times bestsellers, lives in Southern California with his wife, Gerda, their golden retriever, Elsa, and the enduring spirit of their goldens, Trixie and Anna.<br /> <br />Facebook: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://Facebook.com/DeanKoontzOfficial">Facebook.com/DeanKoontzOfficial</a><br />Twitter: @DeanKoontz<br />Website: DeanKoontz.com </span>
